[03/07/23, 7:40:20â€¯PM] Hemant Mohapatra Lightspeed Ai: I'm sure there are several investors looking at this category so, for the right team, there should be plenty of interest. I've been looking at MLops startups across India and US since 2017 but frankly it's a tough area with plenty of OSS tools and no big success after a decade of trying. From Michelangelo to fblearner to airflow to various cloud native tools and 100s more, the MLOps category unfortunately got too fragmented based on different tastes of different data science teams. What's interesting about LLMops is that it seems to be much closer and integrated to the enterprise data stack today than ML ever was -- and therefore likely to align to more standardized practices -- so I'm more hopeful for this category. We've made one adjacent investment here at lightspeed and happy to see more.

[03/07/23, 9:47:12â€¯PM] Sumod Mohan Ai: 2c: We had used DVC and datalad at different times. Datalad is great if you use git-annex, it is great for data provence, model tracking etc but no flows, atleast that is my memory. One click etc were mostly thin layers on top. Automated gsheets sheets (basically touch a particular cell and an experiment is automatically run on cloud). Over time we figured out Excel sheets were best for our usecase, this is about about 20+ projects + upto few 1000 experiments per project. Excel sheet was great because we could pivot on important characteristics etc. We sort of had the feel of what Quora described about their CI/CD deployment, people doing all sorts of experiments now that it was super easy to do. DVC also supports basic flows etc. Don't know enough about details FBlearner's  flows to compare.

[11/08/23, 9:56:08â€¯AM] Pratyush Choudhury Together.Fund Ai: This is something I have been debating a lot w/ a lot of people for a long time. Sharing some pointers - 

1/ Dogfooding can only get you so far - the best companies will have to have a strong, defensible PMF. That unfortunately cannot be replicated very easily everywhere 

2/ There is a difference in $xM in revenue and $xM in PMF revenue - revenue milestones alone might not be enough for next fundraising rounds for startups

3/ The way you define ROI is different as per geographies - for instance, if you're selling to developers, 2 hours of productivity gains for a dev in India â‰  what one would get for the same gains for a US-based dev. This impacts pricing, business models and a lot of downstream things. And I am not at all saying one is any better than the other

[20/08/23, 9:10:10â€¯PM] Shashwatv1 Consumableai: Based on the available information, here are the industries that are expected to experience the highest growth in the next 5 years:

1. Healthcare Predictive Analytics
- The healthcare predictive analytics market is expected to increase at a CAGR of nearly 25% to reach $33 billion in 2026[1].
- The growth is driven by factors such as increasing healthcare needs, aging populations, advancements in medical technology, and the demand for innovative treatments and therapies.

2. Solar Energy Solutions
- The global solar energy solutions market is projected to rise from $30.06 billion in 2022 to reach $62.97 billion by 2027, growing at a CAGR of 15.94%[1].
- The growth is driven by increasing environmental awareness and technological advancements.

3. Virtual Reality Gaming
- The virtual reality gaming market is expected to grow rapidly, with a projected market size of USD 70.57 billion by 2026, growing at a CAGR of 30.2%[2].
- The growth is driven by factors such as increasing demand for immersive gaming experiences, advancements in VR technology, and the growing popularity of e-sports.

4. 5G Security
- The 5G security market is expected to grow rapidly, with a projected market size of USD 9.17 billion by 2026, growing at a CAGR of 44.3%[1].
- The growth is driven by the increasing adoption of 5G technology, which requires advanced security solutions to protect against cyber threats.

5. Digital Education
- The digital education market is expected to grow rapidly, with a projected market size of USD 33.2 billion by 2025, growing at a CAGR of 33.1%[1].
- The growth is driven by factors such as increasing demand for online learning, advancements in educational technology, and the growing popularity of e-learning platforms.

It's important to note that these rankings may vary depending on different sources and methodologies. Additionally, other industries such as e-commerce, information technology, and personal care and service are also expected to experience significant growth in the next 5 years[3].

[28/08/23, 10:17:45â€¯AM] Pratyush Choudhury Together.Fund Ai: Let me elaborate a little bit here - 

1/ If one is in a new category creation play, $1M ARR is seldom enough to get to the next round. What (good) people care about is if they have solid metrics to show they're either at or on track to PMF. 

For instance, if you're trying to build a new category (essentially a new way of doing specific dev workflows) & you have $1.5M ARR coming from India/SEA/Bangalore/Koramangala & Indiranagar, you might find it hard to secure the next round of funding

2/ If you're playing in an existing category (where the budgets and buyer personas are well defined), you'll likely not need that much capital to get to $1M ARR - this is of course assuming you'd deploy some capital efficient GTM strategies &/or some underlying platform shifts

[28/08/23, 11:15:44â€¯AM] Hemant Mohapatra Lightspeed Ai: Interesting discussion :) As a VC who's deployed capital into the US (a16z) and now in India+SEA (Lightspeed), here are my learnings:
1/ if you want to understand a fund's strategy on check size, valuations, ownership, ask not what they believe is the "right" answer, ask for their fund size ğŸ˜‰ 
2/ biggest mental block in genAI founders in india vs US - x raised 100M, why can I only raise 10M. Trust me, if a transformer paper author or CPO of a public market co starts a new company from india, the indian VCs will likely pool together that 100M too. 
3/ ambition matters -- how will the world change fundamentally if you were to succeed? Relative to the US, most founders especially in India market struggle to do this part right but it's critical at the seed/A stage where much of the upside is in the future. Trust me, I know most founders will say 'but.. but.. my idea was the most ambitious thing since chandrayan2' but there is no excuse today if you are on twitter to not be self-aware and intellectually honest..
4/ as a CEO (and i speak as a failed founder in past life), one has only 3 jobs: raise capital (from VCs if you are looking to build a venture outcome; otherwise there are other alternatives better suited), hire the best team, and set strategy. At the start lots of other execution related stuff will bleed into your role, but eventually these 3 are where biggest alpha concentrates over time. 
5/ on the "raising capital" bit, the best founders in every markets *can* raise top decile round sizes; again to know what top decile is depends on total AUM available in and for that market and comparing two different markets without that nuance is purely an academic exercise. The however inverse is not true -- we've also seen best founders start with small rounds (e.g. acceldata seed was < 3m in 2018-19 -- not too long ago; they've raised 90m total). 
6/ no amount of capital can help you reach pmf if you don't know how to get there but it certainly buys you more time; devtools need more bake time but interestingly enough also have least correlation to success vs capital raised; Our portco supabase has raised a ton but barely touched much of that capital e.g.
7/ so, what should you raise? it's like asking what should the stock price for a company be worth -- it is worth what the market thinks it is worth; period... as a founder if you can convince the market it's worth high, great and congrats for having that rare talent - now go and execute behind that promise or you are just pushing eventual death out by another few years that's all; and if you can't convince the market today of that, no worries -- a majority of companies that eventually broke out played the long game and kept building. 

one person's view but happy to die on most of these hills :)

This has been clear for a long time. Most people driven by intellectual curiosity have been engaging with this technology since before the recent spate of Gen AI breakthroughs brought the wide-reaching consequences of this technology into the popular consciousness. 

In our case, we have been investing in Indian AI companies since the firm was established in 2018. We've funded OSS frameworks and dev tools companies (Jovian in 2018 pre-pivot, Embedchain 2023), research labs (Maya Labs 2020), and vertical product companies (skit.ai 2018, Neodocs 2020, consumr.ai 2022, to name just a few). 

Going forward, we will be investing in the same categories, with further interest in teams taking an AI-forward approach to life sciences and materials sciences. Teams like PopVax in Hyderabad, which are leveraging the vast potential of AlphaFold and genAI to design next gen therapies and vaccines.

I see lots of familiar faces and contacts on this group, many of whom have backgrounds in different fields of the tech ecosystem: fintech, ed-tech, health-tech, and yes even crypto. I know that the same thing that attracted them to those industries has brought them here: a curiosity and will to learn about the important technologies that will permeate and improve the world. I hope that you will not pigeon-hole people based on their previous work because I assume everyone is here for the same reasons: to learn, share, and contribute to the domestic ecosystem

[02/10/23, 10:13:47â€¯AM] Shikhil Kumar Gupta Ai: How's small company gonna handle below concern?

Officially no faang company will use the open chatpt or generative ai solutions but rather enterprise versions where data governance, compliance  and security concerns are claimed to be taken care by the enterprises and getting more tighter ,they are made legally bound for penalties also in some cases of breach. Companies need to put enough guardrails and do due diligence before adopting generative ai at scale.

ğŸ” The recently released IndiaAI Expert Group Report is a comprehensive document that outlines the roadmap for AI development in India. It features seven working groups, each focusing on a crucial aspect of AI, from Centres of Excellence to AI Chipsets.

ğŸŒŸ Key Highlights:
1ï¸âƒ£ Centres of Excellence: Aiming to establish three CoEs that will serve as hubs for AI research and development. These CoEs will focus on foundational research and aim to commercialize R&D outputs.
2ï¸âƒ£ India Dataset Platform: Creating a centralized data platform to streamline data collection and utilization across sectors. The platform will act as a PaaS or AaaS, ensuring data quality and enabling efficient data utilization for AI applications.
3ï¸âƒ£ Institutional Capacity: Laying the groundwork for the National Data Management Office (NDMO), which will serve as a nodal agency for data governance and management.
4ï¸âƒ£ Future Design: Providing a comprehensive framework that addresses governance, ethics, and societal impact, shaping the future of AI in India.
5ï¸âƒ£ FutureSkills: Developing a comprehensive AI curriculum to prepare the Indian workforce with the requisite skillsets in AI.
6ï¸âƒ£ Future Labs Compute: Focusing on the challenges and opportunities associated with expanding AI compute capacity in India.
7ï¸âƒ£ AI Chipsets: Offering financial incentives and design infrastructure support to domestic companies and start-ups for the development of AI chipsets.

[30/10/23, 1:20:15â€¯PM] â€ª+91Â 99000Â 64551â€¬: My two cents on virtual address:

We took virtual address in Bangalore during start of covid.
1) First provider could not provide proper documents we needed for ROC + GST. So, we faced a lot of hassle. Finally, had to find immediate replacement.
2) Second provider was pretty good and smooth experience.
3) I moved to Punjab a year ago. And decided to move the company registration to my permanent address to save virtual address rent. I had to spend a lot of money to move registration between states.

My suggestion to use permanent address instead of virtual address to save rent.

[08/12/23, 7:07:27â€¯AM] Keshav CRO E2E: A recent event had a talk on AI and Cloud policies around the world. The discussion included important new developments in the European Union, the US, and India, as well as the bigger picture of global data control and some new policies that will affect it. Quick Notes


1. Indiaâ€™s Stance on AI & Cloud:
    1. Evolution of Digital India Act:Â The Digital India Act is evolving over the IT Act of 2000, the same is in its drafting stage, signaling a shift in India's approach to regulating AI.Â 
    2. Guidelines over Strict Implementation:Â India is likely to adopt guidelines rather than strict implementation for AI development. This approach aligns with the need for flexibility in adapting to evolving regulatory frameworks.
    3. Cloud Regulation Challenges:Â Challenges in cloud regulation, particularly the lack of clear distinctions between Infrastructure as a Service (IAAS), Platform as a Service (PAAS), and Software as a Service (SAAS), highlight potential hurdles for our cloud-based services in the Indian market.SingaporeÂ is soon to release a policy segregating each service, it will be crucial to track the same.
    4. TRAI's Control Attempt:Â The Telecom Regulatory Authority of India (TRAI) proposed consolidating control over cloud and telecom policies under a single regulatory body, suggesting the integration of cloud policies under the Department of Telecommunications (DoT). However, this proposal was rejected, and the response indicated that the regulation of cloud services would remain under the purview of the Ministry of Electronics and Information Technology (MeitY).
    5. Meity Secretary:Â The MeitY Secretary is closely overseeing the forthcoming policies, with a specific focus on those being introduced by Asian countries. This is noteworthy as our Digital India Act is anticipated to encompass a combination of these policies.


2. US Executive Order on AI:
    1. Transparency and Safety: The U.S. is moving towards increased transparency from AI companies, reflecting a global trend.Â DevelopersÂ are encouraged to share safety test results to assess the risks associated with their technology, highlighting the importance of responsible AI development.
    2. Adaptive Policy:Â The policy remains adaptive and flexible, indicating that the regulatory landscape is still evolving.

3. EU AI Policy:
    1. Final Chapter Pending: The EU AI policy is in its final stages, with a proposed motion expected at the end of this week.Â 
    2. Resistance from Key EU Members: France, Italy, and Germany, the initial contributors to the policy's formulation, are currently opposing certain elements of the policy. They are championing self-regulation in order to safeguard and foster their AI entities like Mistral Ai and Aleph Alpha, while also aiming to prevent potential dominance by U.S. entities in the AI ecosystem.
    3. Focus on Human Rights:Â The policy places a strong emphasis on human rights considerations, aligning with the growing global awareness of ethical AI practices.Â 
    4. GDPR Update: An update to the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) of 2018 is anticipated, emphasising limited retention periods for personal data. This aligns with the broader trend of heightened data protection measures globally.
    5. Liability and Accountability Frameworks:Â AI policies likely to emphasize the importance of human oversight and control, especially in critical applications. Users may be granted the right to comprehend when AI-driven decisions are made and possess the ability to intervene or appeal such decisions. Additionally, developers will typically be mandated to establish transparent liability and accountability frameworks, delineating responsibilities and potential repercussions in the event of system failures or adverse consequences. These measures aim to ensure ethical AI practices, user empowerment, and accountability throughout the development and deployment lifecycle.


4. Suggestion over Glocalizing Data for AI:

1. Free Migration of Data:Â The proposition advocating for the unrestricted movement of data across borders signals a significant paradigm shift in data governance. This perspective acknowledges the increasing significance of seamless data flow in global AI strategies.Â 
2. Enforcement and Policing:Â A debate on policing of data was carried with a conclusion that the counsel to enforce and police data should be done selectively and only when necessary furthering a pragmatic and nuanced approach to data governance. Rather than imposing rigid regulations universally, this recommendation suggests tailoring enforcement measures based on specific contextual needs. This approach strikes a balance between ensuring data security and facilitating innovation in the AI landscape. It allows for flexibility in adapting to diverse regulatory environments while addressing concerns related to privacy, security, and ethical use of data.
3. Geofencing of Data:Â The discussion pertained that geofencing of data adds an additional layer of consideration. Geofencing involves establishing virtual perimeters around geographical areas, regulating data based on location. Incorporating geofencing aligns with the idea of selective enforcement, where data governance measures can be tailored according to specific geographic regions in order to comply with specific domestic privacy laws within a state or country.

5. NIST Framework on AI & Cloud:Â It was suggested to monitor the National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) framework for AI & cloud-based policy, given its scientific approach to policy drafting. Similar elements have been incorporated in the executive order on AI by the U.S. and the same is referred to for any amendments.
*-Akash Mor, VP Operations - E2E Cloud.*

[25/12/23, 8:17:16â€¯AM] Deepak Ai: Have spent some time exploring this space. My take: call centres will consolidate further, small call centres will be disrupted by large ones. Large call centres won't be disrupted by tech companies. Thought process: 1) Call centres already have distribution, they'll be able to build tech(which is very much democratized rn) sooner than tech companies build distribution 2) Mostly processes are outsourced, not just calls. And to complete the process you'll always need humans to fill up for edge cases and escalation, which these call centres already have.
Overall: good small space if you're building tech for call centres, not a viable space if you want to replace call centres. 
Observing this space, let's see how this goes.
